      GIFT-E VAL : A B ENCHMARK FOR G ENERAL T IME S E -
002   RIES F ORECASTING M ODEL E VALUATION
003

005    Anonymous authors
006    Paper under double-blind review
007
008
009                                               A BSTRACT
010
011            Time series foundation models excel in zero-shot forecasting, handling diverse
012            tasks without explicit training. However, the advancement of these models has
013            been hindered by the lack of comprehensive benchmarks. To address this gap, we
014            introduce the General TIme Series ForecasTing Model Evaluation, GIFT-Eval,
015            a pioneering benchmark aimed at promoting evaluation across diverse datasets.
016
               GIFT-Eval encompasses 23 datasets over 144,000 time series and 177 million data
               points, spanning seven domains, 10 frequencies, multivariate inputs, and predic-
017
               tion lengths ranging from short to long-term forecasts. To facilitate the effective
018
               pretraining and evaluation of foundation models, we also provide a non-leaking
019            pretraining dataset containing approximately 230 billion data points. Additionally,
020            we provide a comprehensive analysis of R: [20] baselines, which includes statistical
021            models, deep learning models, and foundation models. We discuss each model in
022            the context of various benchmark characteristics and offer a qualitative analysis that
023            spans both deep learning and foundation models. We believe the insights from this
024            analysis, along with access to this new standard zero-shot time series forecasting
025            benchmark, will guide future developments in time series foundation models.
026
027
      1   I NTRODUCTION
028
029
      The success of foundation model pretraining in language and vision modalities has catalyzed similar
030   progress in time series forecasting. By pretraining on extensive time series datasets, a universal
031   forecasting model can be developed, equipped to address varied downstream forecasting tasks across
032   multiple domains, frequencies, prediction lengths, and number of variates in a zero-shot manner (Woo
033   et al., 2024; Rasul et al., 2023b; Ansari et al., 2024).
034
      A critical aspect of foundation model research is creating a high-quality benchmark that includes
035
      large, diverse evaluation data, and preferably non-leaking pretraining data to fairly evaluate models
036   and identify their weaknesses. Research in Natural Language Processing (NLP) has produced key
037   benchmarks such as GLUE, MMLU, etc. (Wang et al., 2018; Hendrycks et al., 2020; Srivastava et al.,
038   2022; Chen et al., 2021), which are crucial for developing high-quality models.
039
      Unlike NLP, time series foundation models lack a unified, diverse benchmark for fair comparison.
040
      For instance, Woo et al. (2024) introduces LOTSA, which remains the largest collection of time series
041
      forecasting pre-training data to date. However, the proposed architecture, Moirai, is evaluated
042   on existing benchmarks that are tailored to specific forecasting tasks, such as the LSF (Zhou et al.,
043   2020) dataset for long-term forecast, and the Monash (Godahewa et al., 2021) dataset for univariate
044   forecasts. Both datasets lack sufficient diversity in time series characteristics and forecasting tasks,
045   making it challenging to evaluate the zero-shot capabilities of foundation models in handling broad
046   and generalized forecasting scenarios. This limitation remains in the recent empirical evaluations of
047   other foundation models, including those featured in benchmarks such as TimesFM, Chronos, and
048   Lag-Llama (Das et al., 2023b; Ansari et al., 2024; Rasul et al., 2023b). Furthermore, the inconsistency
049   in pretraining, training, and test splits across various foundation models complicates comparisons
050
      and poses a risk of data leakage during in-domain and out-of-domain evaluations. To accelerate the
      advancement for research on time series model, it is essential to establish a high-quality and diverse
051
      benchmark that supports universal forecasting evaluation.
052
053   To fill identified gaps, we introduce the General TIme Series ForecasTing Model Evaluation (GIFT-
      Eval), consisting of distinct pretraining and train/test components. The pretraining component


                                                         1

061
062
      features 88 datasets including 240 billion data points (Appendix E lists more details on pretraining
063
      data). The train/test component features 23 datasets encompassing 144,000 time series and 177
064
      million data points across seven domains and 10 frequencies, with prediction lengths ranging from
065   short to long-term, as well as univariate and multivariate forecasting settings. Prior to our work, Qiu
066   et al. (2024) introduced TFB, a comprehensive dataset for time series forecasting. While it offered
067   diversity in the number of variates and domains, it lacks the evaluation of foundation models and
068   accompanying pretraining data without leakage. Our benchmark fills these gaps and it also includes a
069   broader range of frequencies, a more diverse taxonomy, and a wider span of prediction lengths. We
070   compare GIFT-Eval with other similar benchmarks in Table 1. Our contributions are three-fold:
071
      • GIFT-Eval: We introduce a general time series forecasting benchmark that evaluates the zero-shot
072
        and universal forecasting capabilities of foundation models. We provide pretraining and train-test
073
        components that ensure diversity across multiple characteristics and time series features.
074
075
      • Comprehensive Benchmarking: We design diverse forecasting tasks and evaluate R: [20 base-
        lines] that encompass statistical, deep learning, and foundational models on GIFT-Eval.
076
077   • Detailed Analysis: We provide insights into the strengths of different models on all aspects of
078
        GIFT-Eval including domains, frequencies, prediction lengths, and the number of variates R: [and
        also among 6 time series features]. We further provide a qualitative analysis showing failure cases
079
        of both deep learning and foundation models. We believe these insights will contribute to the future
080
        development of foundation models.
081
082
083   2     R ELATED WORK
084
085   Forecasting Methods Time series forecasters can be broadly categorized into statistical models,
086
      deep learning models, and, more recently, foundation models. Statistical models rely solely on
      historical data statistics to predict future values. Among these, ARIMA (Box & Pierce, 1970),
087
      ETS (Hyndman et al., 2008), Theta (Garza et al., 2022), and VAR (Godahewa et al., 2021) are some
088
      of the most widely used ones. With the advent of deep learning technologies, models that apply
089   these techniques to time series forecasting have emerged. Examples include DeepAR (Flunkert
090   et al., 2017), N-BEATS (Oreshkin et al., 2019), and DLinear (Zeng et al., 2022), which utilize
091   pre-transformer architectures. Additionally, transformer-based models such as PatchTST (Nie
092   et al., 2022), Autoformer (Wu et al., 2021), and Crossformer (Zhang & Yan, 2023) have been
093   developed. R: [Another line of important work are probablistic forecasting models. TimeGrad (Rasul
094   et al., 2021) is an autoregressive probabilistic forecasting model utilizing diffusion probabilistic
095   methods, CSDI (Tashiro et al., 2021) is a time series imputation approach that leverages score-
096   based diffusion models conditioned on observed data. Their predecessor GRU NVP (Rasul et al.,
097
      2020) on the other hand, models multivariate temporal dynamics in time series forecasting using
      an autoregressive deep learning model combined with conditioned normalizing flows.] In the last
098
      few years, foundation models have been proposed, inspired by their success in other modalities like
099
      language and vision. The multivariate Moirai (Woo et al., 2024) forecaster, for instance, is based on
100   an encoder-decoder architecture pretrained on a large dataset. Conversely, Chronos (Ansari et al.,
101   2024) and TimesFM (Das et al., 2023b), R: [and Lag-Llama (Rasul et al., 2023a) are univariate
102   forecasters trained using a decoder-only model. Following these other foundation models have also
103   been proposed Timer (Liu et al., 2024), UniTS(Gao et al., 2024), TTM (Ekambaram et al., 2024),
104   Moment (Goswami et al., 2024), and VisionTS (Chen et al., 2024).] However, the main bottleneck in
105   building and evaluating these foundation models is the lack of a diverse and large benchmark dataset.
106
107   Forecasting Benchmarks To address this challenge, several efforts have been made to develop
      extensive time series benchmarks. Woo et al. (2024) introduced LOTSA, which holds the title for


                                                                                  2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      the largest collection of open time series datasets, encompassing 231 billion data points across nine
109   domains. Despite its vast size, the evaluation datasets reuse existing benchmarks from the time series
110   forecasting community and still lack sufficient variety in terms of time series data characteristics and
111   forecasting tasks, which our benchmark aims to augment. Ansari et al. (2024) developed a dataset
112   specifically structured for pretraining, in-domain evaluation, and zero-shot evaluation splits. However,
113   their work is constrained by a limited range of prediction lengths (from 6 to 56), which excludes
114   long-term forecasts, and it restricts the data to univariate forecasting. In contrast, our benchmark
115   encompasses extensive multivariate scenarios and evaluates diverse data across various domains
116   and frequencies. The corpus by Rasul et al. (2023b) presents a diverse array of domains, yet it
117
      comprises only univariate datasets totaling 8,000 time series. In contrast, GIFT-Eval dramatically
      expands this scope with 144,000 time series, enhancing the breadth and depth of the dataset. The
118
      benchmark by Qiu et al. (2024) is closely aligned with our work in its aim to curate a diverse and
119
      comprehensive set of data. However, it lacks pretraining data, does not evaluate foundation models,
120   and limits the taxonomy to time series features only. Our benchmark not only includes pretraining
121   data (with zero-shot evaluation support) but also provides evaluations for foundation models and
122   offers a taxonomy over both characteristics and time series properties. In summary, our benchmark,
123   GIFT-Eval, builds upon and seeks to address the gaps identified in existing time series forecasting
124   benchmarks. We provide a wider comparison with more benchmarks in Table 1. By providing a
125   more diverse and extensive dataset, we aim to facilitate the development and evaluation of foundation
126   models in time series forecasting.
127
128   Forecasting Tools R: [Apart from raw benchmarks, there are also frameworks that provide ac-
129   cess to a range of time series datasets. Prophet (Taylor & Letham, 2018), sktime (Löning et al.,
130   2019), TSlib (Wu et al., 2022) are primarily implemented for point forecasting. On the other hand
131   PyTorchTS (Rasul, 2021), GluonTS (Alexandrov et al., 2020b), NeuralForecast1 are Python packages
132
      for probabilistic time series forecasting, with PyTorchTS including more advanced probabilistic
      models based on deep generative models. ProbTS (Zhang et al., 2023), on the other hand, differs from
133
      these by providing insights into point vs. probabilistic forecasts and short- vs. long-term forecasting.
134
      It is the most relevant to our work, as it includes both classical and foundation models. However, our
135   benchmark is larger in scale, as ProbTS incorporates only 12 multivariate datasets, with no univariate
136   datasets included.]
137
138
139   3       GIFT-E VAL
140
141   In this section, we first provide a background on time series forecasting tasks and define key
142   characteristics and features of time series data. We then outline the design decisions behind the
143
      development of GIFT-Eval, concluding with an analysis that highlights the key features of its final
      distribution.
144
145
146   3.1      BACKGROUND
147
148
      We start by defining univariate and multivariate forecasting tasks. After that, we outline the fun-
      damental characteristics of time series datasets which also influenced our data collection process,
149
      including domain, frequency, number of variates, and prediction length. We also introduce time series
150
      features as part of our data analysis.
151
152
      3.1.1      T IME S ERIES F ORECASTING
153
154   Time series forecasting is a task of predicting future values over one (univariate) or more (multivariate)
155   variates given historical (most commonly real-valued) data which is sampled at regular time intervals.
                                                                                                        dyi ×Ti
156   Suppose D = (Y i , Z i )N                                             i      i   i        i
                               i=1 is a dataset of N time series where Y = (y1 , y2 , . . . , yTi ) ∈ R         is
                                                                          i      i   i        i       dzi ×Ti
157   the target time series with dyi variates and Ti time steps and Z = (z1 , z2 , . . . , zTi ) ∈ R         are
158   the set of covaraites with dzi variates. Then the forecasting task can be modeled as the predictive
159   distribution: p(Yt:t+h |Yt−l:t , Zt−l:t+h ) where l is the context length, and h is the forecast horizon.
160   Univariate forecasting is a special case where the target series is univariate (i.e., dyi = 1), no
161
          1
              https://github.com/Nixtla/neuralforecast


                                                           3
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


162
      covariates are used (i.e., Z = ∅), and only the historical values of the target time series are utilized
163   for prediction.
164
165   3.1.2      T IME S ERIES C HARACTERISTICS AND F EATURES
166
167   Characteristics Time series datasets possess inherent characteristics that define their structure,
168   and common patterns observed in the data and even choices of modelling techniques. We believe a
169   universal forecasting model should be able to perform irrespective of the domain from which the data
170
      is sourced, the granularity at which it was sampled, the length of the forecast horizon and whether it
      is univariate or multivariate. Thus in our study, we focus on these four characteristics: (i) Domain,
171
      i.e., the field or industry from which the time series data originates, such as finance, healthcare or
172
      meteorology. The domain often has a direct effect on the nature of patterns. Another crucial aspect is
173   (ii) the frequency of observations, indicating the time intervals at which the data points are recorded –
174   such as hourly, daily, monthly or annually. (iii) Prediction length, or forecast horizon, is the number
175   of future time steps for which predictions are expected. Lastly, (iv) the number of variates pertains
176   to the dimensionality of the time series data. A univariate time series consists of observations of a
177   single variable over time, whereas a multivariate time series involves multiple interrelated variables.
178   The number of variates adds complexity to the modelling process, as models need to account for
179   dependencies among multiple time series. By ensuring diversity across these specific characteristics
180   in our benchmark, we aim to encompass a wide array of real-life scenarios.
181
182   Features Time series features 2 are statistical properties that capture essential characteristics of the
183
      data. We have selected six such properties to analyze our benchmark, grouped into three categories
      based on the aspects they assess, c.f. Appendix B for a detailed explanation and formula of each
184
      feature. First, we chose two metrics for assessing the temporal attributes of each time series:(i) Trend
185
      refers to the progression of the time series, indicating whether the data shows an overall increase,
186   decrease or stability over time, where higher values indicate stronger trends.(ii) Seasonal strength
187   measures the extent to which regular, repeating patterns occur at specific intervals, such as daily
188   cycles in energy consumption, or annual peaks in finance. The higher the value the more repeating
189   patterns the data exhibits. Second, to assess the forecastability of the time series, we included two
190   metrics:(iii) Entropy measures the “forecastability” of a time series, where low values indicate a
191   high signal-to-noise ratio and high values occur when a series is difficult to forecast. (iv) Hurst
192   exponent quantifies the long-term memory or persistence in a time series. It indicates whether future
193   values are likely to be influenced by past trends, revert to the mean, or behave randomly, where higher
194
      values indicate more persistence. Lastly, to understand the regularity and variability within the time
      series, we selected two metrics: (v) Stability assesses the inconsistency of the mean of the time series.
195
      In simpler terms, it can be defined as the variance of the means. Note that, unlike what the name
196
      suggests, lower values indicate more stable data. Finally, (vi) Lumpiness quantifies the variability
197   of the variance across different segments of the time series. A high value of lumpiness indicates
198   significant fluctuations in variability, which can be challenging to model due to the inconsistent
199   behavior of the data.
200
201   3.2      DATASETS
202
203   To evaluate and advance universal time series forecasting methods, we have curated a comprehensive
204   collection of datasets. Our compilation spans a wide array of domains with varying frequencies,
205   numbers of variates, and prediction lengths. This diversity is crucial for assessing the generalization
206   capabilities of forecasting models across different types of time series data. In the following sections,
207
      we provide detailed descriptions of GIFT-Eval and its unique splits, outlining their sources, and key
      properties. We also conduct a detailed analysis on the test data to gain a better understanding of the
208
      datasets’ characteristics and the distribution of time series features.
209
210
      Train/Test Data We curated the train/test portion of GIFT-Eval with 15 univariate and eight
211
      multivariate datasets, spanning sevem domains and 10 frequencies, totaling 144,000 time series
212   and 177 million data points. We adhere to established prediction lengths for well-known datasets
213   like M4 (Makridakis et al., 2018). For other datasets, we establish three prediction settings—short,
214   medium, and long—based on frequency and domain, with medium and long settings extending the
215
         2
             We use the python implementation of tsfeatures library (Garza et al., 2024) to calculate each feature.


                                                               4
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


216
       short-term length by factors of 10 and 15, respectively. To support models without multivariate
217    forecasting, our framework flattens multivariate datasets for broader compatibility. Data is stored in
218    the Arrow format (Richardson et al., 2023), ensuring efficient integration into deep learning pipelines.
219    Our benchmark features 97 unique triplets of dataset, frequency, and length, with aggregated results
220    for each model reported across these configurations. The sources of each dataset used in train/test
221    split can be found in Appendix D.
222
      We structure the evaluation component of our benchmark by dedicating the final 10% of each dataset
223   in train/test portion to testing, with the rest allocated for training. A non-overlapping rolling evaluation
224   method is employed, setting a predetermined number of windows in the test split, each equal to the
225   dataset’s prediction length. The final window of the training data serves as validation for tuning deep
226   learning model hyperparameters.
227
228   Analysis over test data We analyze GIFT-Eval to understand the distribution and characteristics of
229   the time series features across various datasets, for more granular information see Appendix D. Fig-
230   ure 1 illustrates the mean values of each time series features across different dataset characteristics.
231   These heatmaps provide valuable insights into how metrics such as trend, seasonal strength, Hurst
232   exponent, stability, and lumpiness vary across datasets with different domains, frequencies, prediction
233
      lengths, and numbers of variates. This visualization aids in identifying patterns and potential biases
      within the data, ensuring that the benchmark captures a diverse range of time series behaviors. It also
234
      facilitates fine-grained analysis of model performance across varying dataset characteristics, offering
235
      a comprehensive comparison.
236
237    Number of variates: Figure 1(a) depicts that multivariate data exhibit higher stability and lumpiness
238    values, suggesting more fluctuation in variance across different segments, indicating multivariate
239
       time series are more complex and potentially more challenging to model. Conversely, univariate
       series show stronger seasonal strength, reflecting more pronounced and regular repeating patterns,
240
       making them more predictable over certain periods. Note that the metrics on multivariate time series
241
       are calculated individually for each variate and aggregated for each dataset.
242

265
266    Figure 1: Heatmaps depicting mean values of six time series features across different characteristics.
267
268    Prediction Length: Figure 1(b) shows that shorter prediction lengths have higher values for both
269    trend and Hurst metrics. This suggests that time series with shorter forecast horizons exhibit stronger
       directional movements and greater persistence in their trends, making them potentially easier to


                                                                                                    5
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


270
      predict. As the prediction length increases, the trend and hurst values tend to decrease significantly
271   which makes forcasting harder. Notably, the stability values decrease from short to long indicating
272   higher steadiness in long term while lumpiness increases suggesting higher fluctuations in different
273   sections of the data.
274
      Domain: Figure 1(c) reveals distinct patterns in the metrics. The Web/CloudOps, Sales, and Nature
275
      domains exhibit notably high lumpiness, indicating significant fluctuations in variance. This may
276
      reflect the volatile nature of online operations, sales dynamics and weather predictions. On the other
277   hand, Transport shows the highest entropy and lowest trend values, indicating less predictability,
278   likely due to the variable nature of transportation data influenced by numerous external factors. The
279   Econ/Fin domain shows the highest trend values, indicating strong directional movements that may
280   imply clear market trends or economic cycles. Finally, healthcare exhibits the highest Hurst and
281   lowest lumpiness values, suggesting persistence in the data, possibly due to consistent patient trends
282   or medical outcomes over time.
283   Frequency: Figure 1(d) lists frequencties from highest to lowest. Data with very short intervals,
284   such as secondly (S) and minutely (T) exhibit the lowest seasonal strengths and poor steadiness,
285   indicative of the erratic and volatile nature typical at these granular levels. There is a noticeable
286   increase in seasonal strength progressing from secondly and minutely data to hourly (H) and daily
287   (D). Finally, yearly (A) and quarterly (Q) data demonstrate the strongest trends and Hurst values,
288   with notably low lumpiness, suggesting increased persistence and high predictability. Notably, the
289   yearly data lack seasonal strength measurements due to the tsfeatures library not providing seasonal
290   strength for excessively long time series, a limitation commonly observed in low-frequency datasets.
291   Summary: These observations confirm that our benchmark is rich and diverse, representing a broad
292   range of real-life time series scenarios. Our dataset encompasses various characteristics—such as
293   differing levels of trend, seasonality, persistence, volatility, and complexity—across multiple domains
294   and frequencies. For instance, we include data from domains with high volatility and significant
295   fluctuations, as well as data exhibiting strong persistence and stability. We also cover a wide spectrum
296   of frequencies, from high-frequency data with erratic patterns to low-frequency data with strong
297
      trends and greater predictability. In a similar manner, our metrics show diversity across variate
      types and prediction lengths. This diversity ensures that models are tested across various temporal
298
      behaviors, making our benchmark a robust platform for evaluating the general capabilities of unified
299
      models, particularly foundation models in time series forecasting.
300
301
      Pretraining Dataset We have also curated a pretraining dataset aligned with GIFT-Eval that has
302
      71 univariate and 17 multivariate datasets, spanning seven domains and 13 frequencies, totaling 4.5
303   million time series and 230 billion data points. Notably this collection of data has no leakage issue
304   with the train/test split and can be used to pretrain foundation models that can be fairly evaluated on
305   GIFT-Eval. Further details on pretraining dataset can be found in Appendix E.
306
307
308   4   E XPERIMENTS
309
310   In this section, we present the experimental evaluation of GIFT-Eval across various models.
311
312   Models Time series forecasting training and inference may take different forms for different fami-
313   lies of models. Statistical models make predictions by directly analyzing patterns in the historical
314   data without a separate training phase. We incorporate five statistical models in our benchmark:
315
      Naive, Seasonal Naive (Hyndman & Athanasopoulos, 2018), Auto_Arima, Auto_ETS,
      and Auto_Theta (Garza et al., 2022) methods. Deep learning models require training a specific
316
      model instance for each dataset. Representing deep learning, we select 8 models: DeepAR (Flunkert
317
      et al., 2017), TFT (Lim et al., 2019), TiDE (Das et al., 2023a), N-BEATS (Oreshkin et al., 2019),
318   PatchTST (Nie et al., 2022), DLinear (Zeng et al., 2022), Crossformer (Zhang & Yan, 2023)
319   and iTransformer (Liu et al., 2023b). To obtain both point and probabilistic forecasts, we
320   either adapt models using gluonts (Alexandrov et al., 2020b) with a small probabilistic head or
321   implement our own modifications. We conduct an extensive hyperparameter search for each deep
322   learning model, see Appendix A for details. We evaluate four foundation models on our bench-
323   mark: TimesFM (Das et al., 2023b), Chronos (Ansari et al., 2024) available in tiny, small, and base
      sizes, Moirai (Woo et al., 2024) available in small, base, and large sizes and, R: [ Lag-Llama (Ra-


                                                         6
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


324   Table 2: Results on GIFT-Eval aggregated by domain. The best results across each row are bolded,

      sul et al., 2023a), Timer (Liu et al., 2024), TTM (Ekambaram et al., 2024)] , VisionTS (Chen et al.,
339   2024). These models all provide publicly accessible model parameters for direct use. However, it is
340   important to note that pre-training datasets of TimesFM, Chronos, and Moirai exhibit partial
341   data leakage issues for GIFT-Eval. To keep comparison across models fair, in the main paper we
342   report results with public checkpoints for each model. However, since Moirai provides pretraining
343   code, here we pretrain a series of Moirai models using GIFT-Eval’s pretraining split to demonstrate
344   its utility. We empirically investigate the impact of data leakage in Appendix F.3. Further details on
345   model-specific hyperparameters and tuning can be found in Appendix A.
346   For readability concerns, we omit results from Auto_ETS, DLinear and Crossformer models
347   in the main tables, however, the reader may refer to Appendix F for results with all models available.
348   For the same space concerns, we use abbreviations to replace each model in the tables. Here is
349   a list of model→abbreviation pairs for reference: Naive: Nv., Seasonal Naive: S.Nv.,
350   Auto_Arima: A.Ar., Auto_Theta: A.Th.,Auto_ETS: A.ETS, DeepAR: D.AR, TFT:
351   TFT, TiDE: TiDE, N-BEATS: N-B., PatchTST: P.TST, iTransformer: iTr.,DLinear:
352   DLin., Crossformer: C.former, R: [Lag-Llama: L-Llama, Timer:Timer, TTM: TTM,]
353
      TimesFM: T.FM, VisionTS: V.TS, Chronos: Chr., ChronosSmall : Chr.S , ChronosBase :
      Chr.B , ChronosLarge : Chr.L , Moirai: Moi., MoiraiSmall : Moi.S , MoiraiBase : Moi.B ,
354
      MoiraiLarge : Moi.L .
355
356
      Evaluation setting Performance is assessed using two metrics: the Mean Absolute Scaled Error
357
      (MASE) for point forecasts and the Continuous Ranked Probability Score (CRPS) (Gneiting &
358   Raftery, 2007) for probabilistic forecasts (definition of both metrics are in Appendix C), see Ap-
359   pendix F.2 for results with more metrics. To standardize comparison across benchmarks, both metrics
360   are normalized against the Seasonal Naive baseline. To avoid skew from any single dataset, we
361   employ a ‘Rank’ metric that assigns a numerical ranking to each model across all 97 configurations
362   judging by their CRPS score. The average of these ranks is then reported as the final Rank for each
363   model.
364
365   4.1        R ESULTS
366
367
      We present results across five distinct parts. The first four parts aggregate the results by the key
      characteristics that guided the development of our benchmark: domain, prediction length, frequency,
368
      and number of variates, then conclude the section with aggregation of results across all configurations.
369
      For results on all datasets, frequency and prediction length combinations see Tables 22 to 24.
370
371
      Domain | Table 2 The results across various domains demonstrate that foundation models consis-
372   tently outperform both statistical and deep learning models. Notably, the foundation models achieve
373   top performance in most areas, except in the Web/CloudOps domain. As discussed in Section 3.2
374   Web/CloudOps is one of the domains to exhibit the highest lumpiness. This pattern suggests that
375   foundation models may struggle with time series possessing such characteristics. In contrast, deep
376   learning models like PatchTST and iTransformer excel in these challenging domains, possibly
377   indicating a shortfall of the training data used for foundation models in these areas. The comparison
      of different foundation models yields inconsistent conclusions across various domains. We believe

404
405   this inconsistency is related to the pre-training datasets utilized by the different foundation models
406   and the varying contribution ratios associated with them.
407
408
      Prediction length | Table 3 The analysis by prediction length reveals marked differences in perfor-
      mance across various forecast horizons. For short-term forecasts, foundation models, particularly the
409
      Moirai variants, consistently outperform other models, underscoring their robustness in handling
410
      immediate trends and fluctuations. As the prediction length extends to medium and long terms, the
411   performance of TimesFM and Chronos have significantly declined. This is because the decoder-
412   only architecture adopts recursive multi-step forecast strategy, leading to severe accumulation error
413   issue. Meanwhile, deep learning models such as PatchTST demonstrate superior performance
414   on medium and long terms forecast. This trend indicates that the fine-tuning of deeplearning mod-
415   els effectively captures longer-term dependencies, which are crucial for accurate predictions over
416   extended periods. Thus despite the progress in foundational time series research, there remains a
417   notable performance gap between deep learning and foundation models for medium to long-term
418   predictions, suggesting an area ripe for further research.
419
420   Frequency | Table 4 In the analysis of model performance by data frequency, the highest fre-
421
      quency (secondly) is predominantly led by a statistical baseline, Auto_Theta model, indicating
      its strength in rapid, granular trend capture. As the frequency decreases, deep learning models like
422
      iTransformer and TFT begin to assert dominance, particularly for minutely and hourly data
423
      granularities. However, from daily to yearly frequencies, foundation models consistently outperform
424   other approaches, securing the best results across these settings. This pattern suggests that foundation
425   models, with their extensive pretraining, are better equipped to leverage the broader patterns and
426   slower dynamics typical of lower frequency data, enhancing their forecasting accuracy. Conversely,
427   higher frequency data, which often contains more noise and less discernible patterns, poses challenges
428   that foundation models are less suited to address, as evidenced by their performance relative to more
429   specialized deep learning and statistical models.
430
431   Number of variates | Table 5 In multivariate settings, deep learning models, particularly
      PatchTST (best) and iTransformer (second best), stand out by delivering the best scores

445
446   across all evaluated metrics. Moirai outperforms other foundation models, as it is the only model
447   that supports multivariate forecasting. On the other hand, in univariate scenarios, foundation models,
448   especially the large variant of Moirai, demonstrate superior performance over their deep learning
449
      counterparts. This suggests that foundation models, with their broader pretraining on diverse data
      sets, are particularly adept at extracting and leveraging predictive signals from single streams of data.
450
451
      General | Table 6 The comprehensive aggregation of results across the entire benchmark offers
452
      insightful performance distinctions. PatchTST emerges as the most dominant model for MASE and
453
      CRPS metrics, with MoiraiLarge securing the first place within the Rank metric. We also present
454   the number of times each model achieves the best or second best results in Table 7. MoiraiLarge
455   appears most frequently as the best performer, and as the model that appears in top 2 most frequently.
456   The discrepancy between the RANK and MASE or CRPS metrics suggests that certain datasets
457   may disproportionately influence the metric-based results, which is not captured by the ranking-
458   based outcomes. Thus PatchTST offers reliable results across diverse datasets, making it a strong
459   generalist. In contrast, MoiraiLarge delivers better performance on particular cases.
460   Some recent works (Shi et al., 2024a;b; Ansari et al., 2024) have verified the scaling law in time series
461   foundation models (i.e., larger model performs better), however, GIFT-Eval does not consistently
462   support this conclusion.
463
464   4.2           Q UALITATIVE R ESULTS / FAILURE C ASES
465
466   In addition to the quantitative results discussed earlier, we present qualitative analyses by sharing
467   forecasting samples across various datasets using both deep learning and foundation models. For
468   the deep learning models, we selected four representatives: PatchTST and iTransformer, from
469   recent transformer-based architectures, and DeepAR and N-BEATS, which are more traditional deep
470   learning approaches. Regarding foundational models, we included Moirai to represent encoder-
471
      decoder architectures, Chronos as a decoder-only model, and VisionTS due to its unique method
      of representing the time series through image modality. By examining how these models perform on
472
      different datasets, we aim to provide deeper insights into their forecasting behaviors, strengths, and
473
      limitations.
474
475   The plots in Figures 2(a) and 2(b) show forecasts by deep learning models on the multivariate
476   Bizitobs_l2c dataset (hourly, medium-term) and the univariate Solar dataset (ten-minutely, medium-
477
      term). In Figure 2(a) the irregular patterns challenge the models, with only PatchTST getting close
      to capturing some of the regular spikes accurately. DeepAR and N-BEATS perform reasonably but
478
      miss key periodic spikes, while iTransformer, despite its multivariate capability, oversimplifies
479
      the data into a sinusoidal pattern. In Figure 2(b), traditional models handle seasonal data better but

507
508   still tend to underpredict, with N-BEATS producing a flat forecast in the second plot. PatchTST
509   consistently outperforms others in both instances, showing robustness with both regular and irregular
510   series, while iTransformer continues to underperform.
511   The plots in Figures 2(c) and 2(d) show forecasts by foundation models on two univariate datasets:
512   M_DENSE (daily, long-term) and Solar (ten-minutely, medium-term). Figure 2(c) displays varying
513   performance among the foundation models. Chronos shows a clear degradation in performance as
514   the prediction horizon extends, struggling to maintain accuracy over time, while VisionTS captures
515   spikes but misaligns them. Moirai offers smoother, more conservative forecasts, which may result
516   in less sensitivity to extreme events but provide more consistent alignment with the general trend.
517
      In Figure 2(d) VisionTS predicts seasonal peaks but with timing shifts. On the other hand, both
      Moirai and Chronos struggle to capture the well-spaced regularity of the data, missing key trends
518
      altogether. These poor results across all foundation models (see Figure 2(b) vs Figure 2(d)) mirror
519
      the quantitative findings in Section 4.1, i.e. deep learning models outperform foundation models at
520   higher frequencies. For more qualitative examples see Appendix F.4
521
522
523   5          C ONCLUSION
524
525   We introduce GIFT-Eval, a benchmark designed to evaluate time series forecasting models with
526   diversity across four key characteristics: domain, frequency, number of variates, and prediction
527
      length. We ensure additional diversity by verifying six statistical features across temporal attributes,
      forecastability, and regularity. In addition to the train/test dataset, we also provide a pretraining dataset
528
      with no leakage into our evaluation set. With this, we aim to provide the necessary ground for fairly
529
      comparing different families of models, including foundation models, across a diverse benchmark. We
530   conduct comprehensive experiments with R: [20 baselines] encompassing statistical, deep learning,
531   and foundation models. Leveraging our detailed taxonomy, we provide insights into each model’s
532   strengths relative to different characteristics. We also conduct a qualitative analysis highlighting
533   failure cases in both deep learning and foundation models. GIFT-Eval is a comprehensive benchmark
534   with fine-grained taxonomy that we hope will accelerate the development of new foundation time
535   series forecasting models.

859
860   A    E XPERIMENTAL SETUP DETAILS
861
862   Statistical models We utilize the statsforecast (Garza et al., 2022) library to implement all five
863   statistical baselines: Naive, Seasonal Naive, Auto_Theta, Auto_ETS and Auto_Arima.
      Inference is performed on a CPU server equipped with 96 cores. For each dataset, a time limit of


                                                      16
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


864
      one day is set for the statistical model to complete its run. For any model that times out we halt
865   it and replace its results with those from the Seasonal Naive model as a fallback. Given that
866   some datasets in our benchmark are particularly long, we impose a maximum size constraint on each
867   statistical baseline (set to 1000 with our time constraints), truncating the time series to this maximum
868   size.
869
870
                                   Table 8: Hyperparameter search range for deep learning baselines.
871
872    Parameters
                      TiDE
                      num_layers_encoder          num_layers_decoder       hidden_dim               temporal_hidden_dim   decoder_output_dim   dropout_rate      lr
       Search Range   [1,2]                       [1,2]                    [256,512,1024]           [64,128]              [8,16,32]            [0.0, 0.5]        [1e-5:1e-1]
873                   N-BEATS                                                                                                                  PatchTST

877
878
879   Deep learning models For all deeplearning models we either used models readily available in
880   gluonts library (Alexandrov et al., 2020b) or we write our own wrappers. Where feasible we also add
881   a probabilistic forecasting head to the models. Where direct probabilistic outputs are not feasible, we
882
      generate probabilistic evaluations by converting point forecasts into sample forecasts using a single
      sample. To identify the optimal hyperparameters, we conducted a comprehensive search across all
883
      97 runs included in GIFT-Eval. We employed the ray library (Moritz et al., 2017) to parallelize the
884
      search on a single GPU and used the optuna (Akiba et al., 2019) library to extend this parallelization
885   across multiple GPU servers. We search for 15 trials for each deep learning model per each of the 97
886   runs. Table 8 lists the range of parameters we search for each model. On top of the listed parameters
887   for each model, we also search for weight decay on all runs in the range: [1e − 8 : 1e − 2], R: [and
888   for context length in range [1, 2, 4, 8] × prediction_length.] For the Crossformer model on the
889   long term setting of Jena Weather dataset with both ten–minutely and hourly frequencies, we had to
890   limit the search for d_model and n_heads, fixing them at 32 and 1, respectively. This adjustment
891   was necessary because the model’s attention mechanism operates across multiple variates, leading to
892   an OOM (Out of Memory) error due to the high number of variates present in this dataset.
893
894   Foundation models For all foundation models we use their public versions available online and
895
      conduct zero-shot evaluation on our benchmark’s test-split. Since Moirai (Woo et al., 2024) provides
      multi-patch size projections and varying context lengths. We adopt the similar approach by defining
896
      a frequency-to-patch size mapping as follows:
897
898              • Yearly, Quarterly: 8
899
                 • Monthly: 8
900
901              • Weekly, Daily: 16
902              • Hourly: 32
903
                 • Minute-level: 32
904
905              • Second-level: 64
906
      R: [We set context length to 4000].    We used the public available Moirai
907
      models from the corresponding HuggingFace repos, i.e., MoiraiSmall - https:
908
      //huggingface.co/Salesforce/moirai-1.1-R-small,             MoiraiBase    -
909   https://huggingface.co/Salesforce/moirai-1.1-R-base,          MoiraiLarge -
910   https://huggingface.co/Salesforce/moirai-1.1-R-large.
911
912
      For Chronos, we mainly follow their official implementation3 for evaluation: with the num-
      ber of samples as 20. The models are loaded from the corresponding HuggingFace repos, e.g.,
913
      ChronosTiny - https://huggingface.co/amazon/chronos-t5-tiny, ChronosSmall
914
      - https://huggingface.co/amazon/chronos-t5-small, ChronosBase - https://
915   huggingface.co/amazon/chronos-t5-base.

917       https://github.com/amazon-science/chronos-forecasting/blob/main/
      scripts/evaluation/evaluate.py


                                                                                                    17
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


918
      For TimesFM, we follow their official implementation4 for evaluation. We set the context length
919   for evaluation as 512 as mentioned in their paper since the maximum context length in training
920   is 512. Following their default setting in their example, we keep the input patch length as 32,
921   the output patch length as 128, the number of layers as 20, and the model dimension as 1280.
922   TimesFM comes with only one model size, i.e., timesfm-1.0-200m, and we load the model from
923   https://huggingface.co/google/timesfm-1.0-200m.
924
      For VisionTS, we follow their official implementation5 for evaluation. We set the context length as
925   2000, the norm constant as 0.4, the alignment constant as 0.4 according to their default settings. We
926   use their implementation for seasonality detection to generate a candidate list and search an optimal
927   seasonality parameter with the validation data.
928
929   Additional parameters and computational resources. All experiments are conducted on eight
930   NVIDIA A100 GPUs. For models that has gone through training the loss function and optimizer are
931   set following their original implementation. Additionally we set the batch size to 128 and, number of
932   batches per epoch to 100, and finally number of epochs to 50.

      B    D ETAILS OF T IME S ERIES F EATURES
935
936
      This section gives a detailed view of the time series features we used to analyze the test portion
937
      of our data in Section 3.2. We use tsfeatures library (Garza et al., 2024) to calculate each metric.
938   Given the scale of our dataset, we limit each time series history to the most recent 500 data points
939   before computing the respective features. The prediction length remains faithful to the original values
940   specified for the dataset and is not clipped. R: [ Table 9 shows specific time series features of each
941   dataset where we computed specific we classified them based on whether each feature (e.g., trend,
942   seasonality, entropy) was lower or higher than the median value across all datasets].
943
      We also acknowledge that for some overly short time series, tsfeatures may output NaN (Not a
944   Number) values for certain features—for example, the seasonal strength of some yearly time series
945   data. In such cases, we exclude these NaN values during aggregation. Below we provide specific
946   details for each feature used:
947
948   Trend Using the STL (Seasonal and Trend decomposition using Loess) method, a time series xt is
949   decomposed into a trend component ft , multiple seasonal components si,t for i = 1, . . . , M , and a
950   remainder component et :
951
952
                                          xt = ft + s1,t + · · · + sM,t + et ,
953
954   where M is the number of seasonal periods. The strength of the trend is quantified by comparing
955   the variance of the remainder component et to the combined variance of the trend and remainder
956   components. Specifically, the strength of the trend is defined as:
957
958                                                           Var(et )
959                                          trend = 1 −                  .
                                                            Var(ft + et )
960
961   If the calculated value of trend is less than 0, it is set to 0; if it is greater than 1, it is set to 1. This
962   measure indicates the proportion of the variability in the time series that is explained by the trend
963   component, with values closer to 1 signifying a stronger trend.
964
965   Seasonal Strength Following the same decomposition above the strength of each seasonal com-
966   ponent is quantified by comparing the variance of the remainder et to the combined variance of the
967   seasonal component si,t and the remainder.
968   For each seasonal component si,t , the strength of seasonality is defined as:
969
        4
970       https://github.com/google-research/timesfm/blob/master/experiments/
971   long_horizon_benchmarks/run_eval.py
        5
          https://github.com/Keytoyze/VisionTS/blob/main/eval_gluonts/run.py

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1026
1027                                                                      Var(et )
1028                                   seasonal_strengthi = 1 −                         .
                                                                        Var(si,t + et )
1029
1030   If the calculated value of seasonal_strengthi is less than 0, it is set to 0; if it is greater than 1, it is set
1031   to 1. For non-seasonal time series, seasonal_strength = 0. This measure indicates the proportion of
1032   the variability in the time series that is explained by the i-th seasonal component, with values closer
1033   to 1 signifying stronger seasonality for that component.
1034
1035   Entropy     Entropy is defined as the Shannon entropy of the normalized spectral density estimate
1036   fˆ(λ):
1037
                                                           Z π
1038
1039
                                          Entropy = −             fˆ(λ) log fˆ(λ) dλ,
                                                             −π
1040
1041   where fˆ(λ) is an estimate of the spectral density of the data. A lower spectral entropy indicates a
1042   higher signal-to-noise ratio, meaning the time series has more predictable patterns and is easier to
1043   forecast. Conversely, a higher spectral entropy suggests that the series is more complex and difficult
1044   to predict.
1045
1046   Hurst Exponent The Hurst exponent (hurst) is computed as 0.5 plus the maximum likelihood
1047   estimate of the fractional differencing order d by Haslett & Raftery (1989). The addition of 0.5
1048   ensures consistency with the traditional Hurst coefficient. The values of the Hurst exponent vary
1049   between 0 and 1, with higher values indicating a smoother trend, less volatility, and less roughness.
1050
1051   Stability Stability measures the variability of the mean values across all tiles. It is defined as the
1052   variance of the means of the tiled windows. If the time series is divided into N tiles and x̄i represents
1053
       the mean of the i-th tile, then the stability is calculated as:
1054
1055                                       Stability = Var (x̄1 , x̄2 , . . . , x̄N ) .
1056
1057   A lower stability indicates that the means are consistent across tiles, suggesting a stable time series.
1058   A higher stability implies significant differences in means, indicating potential shifts or trends in the
1059
       data.
1060
1061   Lumpiness Lumpiness assesses the variability of the variances across all tiles. It is defined as the
1062
       variance of the variances of the tiled windows. Let s2i denote the variance of the i-th tile. Lumpiness
       is then computed as:
1063
1064
                                          Lumpiness = Var s21 , s22 , . . . , s2N .

1065
1066
1067   A higher lumpiness suggests that the variability within the tiles differs significantly, indicating that
1068
       the time series may have periods of high and low volatility. A lower lumpiness means the variances
       are similar across tiles, pointing to a more homogeneous time series in terms of variability.
1069
1070
1071   C    E VALUATION M ETRICS
1072
1073   We use two metrics to evaluate performance of forecasters: Mean Absolute Scaled Error (MASE) for
1074   point forecasting ability and Continous Ranked Probability Score (CRPS) for probabilistic forecasting.
1075   For both metrics we use gluonts library implementation to calculate final values (Alexandrov et al.,
1076   2020a).
1077
1078   MASE R: [ MASE (Mean Absolute Scaled Error) is an evaluation metric commonly used in time
1079   series analysis to assess forecast accuracy. Unlike metrics such as MAPE, MASE addresses issues of
       scale dependence and sensitivity to outliers. It is defined as the mean absolute error of the forecast Ŷt ,


                                                               20
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1080
       scaled by the mean absolute error of a naïve benchmark forecast, typically a one-step-ahead lag of
1081   the actual values. The formula for MASE is: ]
1082
1083                                                       1
                                                                Pn
                                                           n      t=1   Yt − Ŷt
1084                                        MASE =              Pn                         ,
                                                         1
1085                                                    n−1       t=2 |Yt − Yt−1 |
1086
1087   where:
1088
1089            • Yt is the actual value at time t,
1090
                • Ŷt is the forecasted value at time t,
1091
1092            • n is the number of observations.
1093
1094   MASE is scale-independent, making it suitable for comparing forecast accuracy across different
1095
       time series. A MASE value less than 1 indicates that the forecast performs better than the naïve
       benchmark, while a value greater than 1 indicates worse performance. It is particularly useful in
1096
       scenarios with varying scales or when evaluating the effectiveness of forecasts relative to a simple
1097
       baseline.
1098
1099
1100
       CRPS The Continuous Ranked Probability Score (CRPS) is a metric used in probabilistic forecast-
       ing to evaluate the accuracy of predicted cumulative distribution functions (CDFs) against observed
1101
       values. Given a predicted distribution with CDF F and a ground truth value y, the CRPS is defined
1102
       as:
1103
1104
                                                           Z 1
1105
                                         CRPS(F, y) =            2Λα (F −1 (α), y) dα,
1106                                                        0
1107
1108   where the quantile loss Λα (q, y) is defined as:
1109
1110
1111                                       Λα (q, y) = (α − 1{y < q})(y − q).
1112
1113   In practice, computing the CRPS integral can be computationally intensive. To address this, we
1114   approximate the CRPS using a discrete sum over a finite set of quantile levels. This approximation,
1115   often referred to as the mean weighted quantile loss (Park et al., 2021), is given by:
1116
1117                                                              K
                                                           1 X
1118                                            CRPS ≈         wQL[αk ],
1119
                                                           K
                                                                 k=1
1120
1121   where K is the number of quantile levels, and {α1 , α2 , . . . , αK } are the selected quantile levels (e.g.,
1122   αk = 0.1k for k = 1, 2, . . . , 9 when K = 9).
1123   The weighted quantile loss wQL[α] for each quantile level α is calculated as:
1124
1125                                                        P
1126                                                             t ΛP
                                                                    α (q̂t (α), yt )
                                             wQL[α] = 2                                ,
1127                                                                    t |yt |
1128
1129   where:
1130
1131            • q̂t (α) is the predicted α-quantile at time step t,
1132            • yt is the actual observed value at time t,
1133
                • Λα (q̂t (α), yt ) is the quantile loss at time t for quantile level α.

       D        GIFT-E VAL TEST DATASETS
1135
1136
       In this section we provide comprehensive list of datasets used in test portion of GIFT-Eval along
1137
       with original sources, for details regarding the pretraining portion see Appendix E. We utilize 10
1138   open domain sources to curate the benchmark, here we list each one along with its properties in
1139   detail. We incorporate Jena Weather6 dataset following Autoformer (Wu et al., 2021). We process
1140   BizITObs Application, Service, and L2C7 following the pipeline in AutoMixer (Palaskar et al.,
1141   2024).These datasets consist of business and IT observability data, fusing both business KPIs and IT
1142   event channels into multivariate time series data. Within the same domain we also process Bitbrains
1143   datasets from Grid Workloads Archive (Shen et al., 2015). The Restaurant data is borrowed from
1144   Recruit Restaurant Forecasting Competition (Howard et al., 2017), The task associated with this
1145   dataset is to use reservation and visitation data to predict the total number of visitors to a restaurant for
1146
       future dates. From Informer (Zhou et al., 2021) we utilize ETT1 and ETT2 datasets, which denote
       electricity transformer temperature and serve as an indicator used in the electricity power long-term
1147
       deployment. Datasets for Transport domain are extracted from LibCity (Wang et al., 2023a), which
1148
       provides a collection of urban time series datasets. We utilize the solar dataset from LSTNet (Lai
1149   et al., 2017) where the task is to predict solar plant energy outputs. The second dataset for Sales
1150   data is by Mancuso et al. (2020). Monash (Godahewa et al., 2021) is a large collection of diverse
1151   time series datasets across many domains, we choose a subset of these datasets making sure there
1152   is no leak from pretrain to test split. Finally, from UCI ML Archive (Trindade, 2015) we use the
1153   electricity dataset which contains electricity consumption of 370 individual clients. Table 14 lists
1154   all datasets, along with their source, frequency, prediction length and number of variates setup and
1155   presents various statistics from number of series, to series length, and also number of observations.
1156   We use last 10% of each timeseries in the test portion of our data for testing and keep the rest for
1157
       training.
1158    Tables 10 to 13 present detailed statistics on the number of time series and total observations within
1159   each characteristic category of the test benchmark. Specifically, these tables break down the data by
1160   domain (Table 11), frequency (Table 12), prediction length (Table 10), and variate count (Table 13),
1161   offering a quantitative overview of the dataset’s composition.

1205
1206
1207   E               GIFT-E VAL PRE - TRAINING DATASETS
1208
1209   The pre-training split of GIFT-Eval is constructed based on LOTSA (Woo et al., 2024), and we
1210   excluded certain datasets from it to form part of the evaluation set, making it more diverse and
1211   balanced.
1212
       Here is a brief discussion on each of the used sources: BuildingsBench (Emami et al., 2023) compiled
1213
       datasets on residential and commercial building energy consumption. ClimateLearn (Nguyen et al.,
1214   2023) offered time series of various climate-related variables, including temperature, humidity, and
1215   multiple pressure levels. CloudOps TSF (Woo et al., 2023) introduced large-scale CloudOps time
1216   series datasets that capture key variables such as CPU and memory utilization. GluonTS (Alexandrov
1217   et al., 2020a) provided a variety of datasets commonly used in time series forecasting. LargeST (Liu
1218   et al., 2023a) sourced from the California Department of Transportation Performance Measurement
1219   System (PeMS) to date, which is widely used for traffic forecasting. LibCity (Wang et al., 2023a)
1220   provided a collection urban spatio-temporal datasets. SubseasonalClimateUSA (Mouatadid et al.,
1221   2023) provided climate time series data at daily level. ProEnFo (Wang et al., 2023b) introduced a
1222
       range of datasets for load forecasting which include various covariates such as temperature, humidity,
       and wind speed. Monash (Godahewa et al., 2021) is a large collection of diverse time series datasets,
1223
       the most popular source for building time series foundation models. LOTSA_Others (Woo et al.,
1224
       2024) are complementary datasets collected by LOTSA to enhance the diversity.
1225
1226   The complete list of pre-training datasets and their respective sources, key properties are provided in
1227   Table 15.
1228
1229   F              F INEGRAINED R ESULTS
1230
1231   F.1                R: [R ESULTS AGGREGATED BY T IME S ERIES F EATURES ]
1232
1233   R: [ Table 16 shows results aggregated by time series features. We use the determined features of each
1234   dataset as depicted in Table 9 and aggregated across both high and low property of each feature in
1235   order get these results. The Table reveals a few key insights about model performance under varying
1236   features:]
1237   R: [Temporal Strength (Trend and Seasonality): When datasets exhibit low trend or low seasonality,
1238   deeplearning models such as PatchTST, TFT, and iTransformer generally perform better
1239   compared against foundation models. This may suggest that deep learning models are effective
1240   in handling scenarios with less pronounced temporal dynamics. In contrast, at higher levels of
1241   trend or seasonality, foundation models like MoiraiLarge often show better performance, potentially
       indicating their strength in capturing more regular patterns.]


                                                                                                                                                                   23
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

1292   R: [Entropy and Hurst: High entropy (suggesting greater forecasting difficulty) and low Hurst
1293   (indicating stochasticity thus again high difficulty) appear to favor Transformer-based models (except
1294   for Moirai variants). On the other hand, for lower entropy or higher Hurst values, foundation
1295   models, achieve better results on average, suggesting they may handle simpler temporal structures
       more effectively.]

1316
1317   R: [Stability: When stability is high (i.e. high variance), deep learning models seem to perform
1318   better, while for lower stability (lower variance), foundation models like MoiraiLarge tend to have
1319   an edge. This may indicate that deep learning models excel in highly variable conditions, while
1320   foundation models can handle less complex variance better.]
1321   R: [Overall, these observations suggest that deep learning models, particularly Transformers like
1322   PatchTST, may perform better in more challenging, less predictable scenarios. In contrast, founda-
1323   tion models like MoiraiLarge seem to excel when temporal patterns are simpler and more predictable.
1324   This aligns with the aggregated results across datasets, where PatchTST shows strong generalist
1325   performance, while MoiraiLarge ranks highly in specific, presumably in easier-to-forecast cases.
1326   It is also likely that the supervised learning setup and hyperparameter tuning play a role in these
1327
       differences, potentially giving an advantage to deep learning models in more complex scenarios.]

1330   F.2            R ESULTS WITH ALL MODELS
1331
1332   In this section, we present results for all models, including those omitted from the main paper in
1333   section Section 4.1 due to space constraints. The results are displayed in the same aggregated form
1334
       through Tables 17 to 21. Furthermore, we provide non-aggregated results across all dataset, term and
       frequency combinations in Tables 22 to 24.
1335
1336
1337
       F.3            DATA L EAKAGE E FFECT IN F OUNDATION M ODELS
1338
1339
1340   Due to the use of different pre-training datasets by various foundation models, there is a partial data
1341
       leakage issue when these models are evaluated against our GIFT-Eval. To ensure a fair comparison,
       we pre-trained a new series of Moirai models on our GIFT-Eval pre-training data and report
1342
       the results from these models in the main paper. To further examine the impact of data leakage
1343
       on foundation models, we also include the performance of the original Moirai models on our
1344   benchmark, both to facilitate replicability and to demonstrate the misleading effects that leakage can
1345   introduce. We call the original model Moirai-Leakage and use the abbreviation Moi Leak..
1346   The results for all datasets affected by leakage are provided in Table 25. In most cases, data leakage
1347   from training sets can boost performance on the corresponding test sets, with this effect becoming
1348   more pronounced as prediction length increases. This finding highlights the capacity of foundation
1349   models to memorize training data and underscores the critical importance of preventing data leakage
       when comparing time series foundation models on public benchmarks.


                                                                                                                                             25
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

       F.4          A DDITIONAL Q UALITATIVE E XAMPLES
1383
1384   In addition to the four examples shared in the main paper, we present three additional qualitative
1385   examples in Figure 3. Figure 3(a) illustrates the forecasts of foundation models on the Bizitobs_l2c
1386   dataset (hourly, medium-term). Similar to previous observations, Chronos forecasts tend to degrade
1387   over longer time horizons. Unlike in earlier scenarios, Moirai also shows poor performance on this
1388   dataset, missing all the regular peaks and troughs. In contrast, the VisionTS model provides the
1389   forecast closest to the ground truth. Figure 3(b) presents forecasts for a higher-frequency dataset:
1390   Electricity (15-minute intervals, long-term). Notably, Chronos excels on this dataset, showing
1391   better consistency than both Moirai and VisionTS. While Moirai performs reasonably well, it
1392
       tends to predict some stationary changes (see the rightmost side of the upper plot) that are not aligned
       with the ground truth data. In contrast, VisionTS repeats a mistake observed in earlier datasets by
1393
       predicting shifted peaks. The final plots in Figure 3(c) display forecasts from deep learning models
1394
       on the same Electricity dataset (15-minute intervals, long-term). Compared to the foundation models,
1395   these deep learning models demonstrate poorer performance. Notably, the model that differs most by
1396   its forecast is DeepAR, which quickly flattens at the beginning of the prediction—a phenomenon also
1397   observed with another deep learning model, N-BEATS, in the Solar dataset example in Figure 2(b).
1398

       different variates, making them suitable for evaluating multivariate forecasting capabilities. If these
